武帝紀:
秋七月，以夫人卞氏為王后。遣于禁助曹仁擊關羽。八月，漢水溢，灌禁軍，軍沒，羽獲禁，遂圍仁。使徐晃救之。
In autumn, seventh month, the King made Lady Bian his queen. He dispatched Yu Jin to assist Cao Ren in attacking Guan Yu. In the eighth month, the Han River overflowed and flooded Yu Jin's forces; his army was overwhelmed, Yu Jin captured by Guan Yu, who then laid siege to Cao Ren. He sent Xu Huang to rescue him.

120
Jump to dictionary
武帝紀:
九月，相國鍾繇坐西曹掾魏諷反免。世語曰：諷字子京，沛人，有惑衆才，傾動鄴都，鍾繇由是辟焉。大軍未反，諷潛結徒黨，又與長樂衞尉陳禕謀襲鄴。未及期，禕懼，告之太子，誅諷，坐死者數十人。王昶家誡曰「濟陰魏諷」，而此云沛人，未詳。
In the ninth month, Xiangguo Zhong Yao was dismissed from office for being implicated in a plot by Wei Feng, an official of the Xicao Bureau. The Shiyu states: Feng, courtesy name Zijing, was a native of Pei. He possessed the talent to mislead people and stirred great influence in Ye Du; Zhong Yao therefore appointed him. Before the main army returned, Feng secretly formed a faction and conspired with Chen Yi, the Changle Weiwei, to attack Ye. Before the planned time arrived, Yi became fearful and reported it to the Crown Prince; Feng was executed, along with dozens of others implicated in the plot. Wang Chang's Family Admonitions refer to "Wei Feng of Ji Yin," yet this record calls him a native of Pei; the discrepancy remains unclear.

121
Jump to dictionary
武帝紀:
冬十月，軍還洛陽。曹瞞傳曰：王更脩治北部尉廨，令過於舊。孫權遣使上書，以討關羽自效。王自洛陽南征羽，未至，晃攻羽，破之，羽走，仁圍解。王軍摩陂。魏略曰：孫權上書稱臣，稱說天命。王以權書示外曰：「是兒欲踞吾著爐火上邪！」侍中陳羣、尚書桓階奏曰：「漢自安帝已來，政去公室，國統數絕，至於今者，唯有名號，尺土一民，皆非漢有，期運乆已盡，歷數乆已終，非適今日也。是以桓、靈之閒，諸明圖緯者，皆言『漢行氣盡，黃家當興』。殿下應期，十分天下而有其九，以服事漢，羣生注望，遐邇怨歎，是故孫權在遠稱臣，此天人之應，異氣齊聲。臣愚以為虞、夏不以謙辭，殷、周不吝誅放，畏天知命，無所與讓也。」魏氏春秋曰：夏侯惇謂王曰：「天下咸知漢祚已盡，異代方起。自古已來，能除民害為百姓所歸者，即民主也。今殿下即戎三十餘年，功德著於黎庶，為天下所依歸，應天順民，復何疑哉！」王曰：「『施於有政，是亦為政』。若天命在吾，吾為周文王矣。」曹瞞傳及世語並云桓階勸王正位，夏侯惇以為宜先滅蜀，蜀亡則吳服，二方旣定，然後遵舜、禹之軌，王從之。及至王薨，惇追恨前言，發病卒。孫盛評曰：夏侯惇恥為漢官，求受魏印，桓階方惇，有義直之節；考其傳記，世語為妄矣。
In winter, tenth month, the army returned to Luoyang. The Biography of Cao Man says: The King renovated and repaired the office of the northern commandery warden, making it more elaborate than before. Sun Quan sent envoys with a letter, offering to campaign against Guan Yu as proof of his loyalty. The King personally led an army from Luoyang to campaign south against Guan Yu, but before he arrived, Xu Huang attacked and defeated Guan Yu; Guan Yu fled, lifting the siege on Cao Ren. The King's army stationed at Mobei. The Wei Lue states: Sun Quan submitted a letter acknowledging himself as a vassal, citing the Mandate of Heaven in his address. The King showed Sun Quan's letter to those outside and said: "Does this child want me to sit on the fire?" Shizhong Chen Qun and Shangshu Huan Jie presented a memorial stating: "Since Emperor An of Han, the government has drifted from the imperial house. The line of succession has repeatedly been broken; up to now, there is only the title remaining. Not an inch of land or a single subject belongs to Han anymore. Its destiny and mandate have long since ended—it is not merely a matter of today." Therefore, during the reigns of Emperor Huan and Emperor Ling, those who studied omens and prophecies clearly stated: 'The Han dynasty's vital energy has run out; it is time for the Huang family to rise.'" "Your Highness has fulfilled the destined time, holding nine-tenths of the realm. Yet you still serve Han, which causes all living beings to look forward with expectation and elicits sighs from near and far. Hence, Sun Quan, though distant, submits as a vassal—this is the harmony between Heaven and humanity, where different forces resonate in unison." "As humble subjects, we believe that Yu and Xia did not hesitate to accept their mandate through modesty, nor did Yin and Zhou refrain from punishing or deposing rulers. Revering Heaven and understanding destiny, there is nothing left to yield." The Spring and Autumn Annals of the Wei Dynasty states: Xiahou Dun said to the King, "All under heaven know that the Han dynasty's mandate has ended; a new era is about to begin. Since ancient times, those who have removed the people's hardships and gained their allegiance are the true leaders of the people. "Your Highness has been in arms for over thirty years, with virtue and merit evident among the common people. You are relied upon by all under heaven; to follow Heaven's will and the people's wishes—what is there left to doubt!" The King said: " 'To exert influence in governance, this too is governance.' " "If Heaven's mandate belongs to me, then I am merely the Duke of Zhou." Both the Biography of Cao Man and the Shiyu state that Huan Jie advised the King to assume the throne, while Xiahou Dun believed they should first destroy Shu. Once Shu fell, Wu would submit; after both regions were pacified, then follow the example of Emperor Shun and Yu. The King followed this advice. When the King passed away, Xiahou Dun regretted his previous words deeply and died from illness. Sun Sheng commented: Xiahou Dun was ashamed to serve as an official of Han and sought the seal of Wei. Huan Jie, in comparison with Xiahou Dun, demonstrated a sense of righteousness and integrity; Examining their biographies, the Shiyu is clearly false.

122
Jump to dictionary
武帝紀:
二十五年春正月，至洛陽。權擊斬羽，傳其首。
In the first month of spring in the twenty-fifth year [of reign], he arrived at Luoyang. Sun Quan attacked and beheaded Guan Yu, sending his head as a trophy.

123
Jump to dictionary
Show parallel passages
武帝紀:
庚子，王崩于洛陽，年六十六。世語曰：太祖自漢中至洛陽，起建始殿，伐濯龍祠而樹血出。曹瞞傳曰：王使工蘇越徙美棃，掘之，根傷盡出血。越白狀，王躬自視而惡之，以為不祥，還遂寑疾。遺令曰：「天下尚未安定，未得遵古也。葬畢，皆除服。其將兵屯戍者，皆不得離屯部。有司各率乃職。斂以時服，無藏金玉珍寶。」謚曰武王。二月丁卯，葬高陵。
On the Gengzi day, the King died in Luoyang at the age of sixty-six. The Shiyu says: Since returning from Hanzhong to Luoyang, the Taizu built the Jianshi Palace and felled a tree at Zhuolong Temple, which bled blood. The Biography of Cao Man records: The King ordered the craftsman Su Yue to move a beautiful pear tree; when they dug it up, its roots were injured and blood flowed out. Yue reported the incident, and the King personally inspected it but was horrified by what he saw. He considered it an ill omen and soon fell seriously ill upon returning. In his last will, the King said: "The realm is not yet at peace; I cannot follow ancient customs. After the funeral rites are completed, all shall remove their mourning attire. Those commanding troops and stationed for garrison duty must not leave their posts. Officials in charge shall each perform their duties as assigned. "Wrap my body in regular clothing; do not bury gold, jade, or treasures with me." He was posthumously honored as Emperor Wu. In the second month, on the Dingmao day, he was buried in Gaoling Mausoleum.

124
Jump to dictionary
武帝紀:
魏書曰：太祖自統御海內，芟夷羣醜，其行軍用師，大較依孫、吳之法，而因事設奇，譎敵制勝，變化如神。自作兵書十萬餘言，諸將征伐，皆以新書從事。臨事又手為節度，從令者克捷，違教者負敗。與虜對陣，意思安閑，如不欲戰，然及至決機乘勝，氣勢盈溢，故每戰必克，軍無幸勝。知人善察，難眩以偽，拔于禁、樂進於行陣之間，取張遼、徐晃於亡虜之內，皆佐命立功，列為名將；其餘拔出細微，登為牧守者，不可勝數。是以刱造大業，文武並施，御軍三十餘年，手不捨書，晝則講武策，夜則思經傳，登高必賦，及造新詩，被之管絃，皆成樂章。才力絕人，手射飛鳥，躬禽猛獸，甞於南皮一日射雉獲六十三頭。及造作宮室，繕治器械，無不為之法則，皆盡其意。雅性節儉，不好華麗，後宮衣不錦繡，侍御履不二采，帷帳屏風，壞則補納，茵蓐取溫，無有緣飾。攻城拔邑，得靡麗之物，則悉以賜有功，勳勞宜賞，不吝千金，無功望施，分豪不與，四方獻御，與羣下共之。常以送終之制，襲稱之數，繁而無益，俗又過之，故預自制終亡衣服，四篋而已。
The Records of Wei state: Since the Taizu took control over all under heaven, he eliminated his enemies. In leading military campaigns and employing troops, he generally followed Sun Tzu and Wu Qi's methods, yet devised ingenious strategies according to circumstances, outwitting the enemy and securing victory with changes as miraculous as divine intervention. He personally composed a military treatise of over one hundred thousand words; all generals on campaign followed its principles in their operations. When matters arose, he personally issued detailed instructions. Those who obeyed his orders achieved victory; those who disobeyed suffered defeat. When facing the enemy in battle, he appeared calm and composed as if uninterested in fighting. Yet when it came to decisive moments of seizing victory, his momentum was overwhelming; thus, every battle ended in triumph, with no success due to mere luck. He was skilled at recognizing talent and difficult to deceive by pretense. He promoted Yu Jin and Yue Jin from among the ranks, selected Zhang Liao and Xu Huang from captured enemies, all of whom assisted in founding his dynasty and achieved great feats, becoming renowned generals; Others who were discovered among the obscure and elevated to positions as governors or commandery administrators could not be counted in full. Therefore, in founding his great cause, he applied both civil and military strategies. For over thirty years commanding the army, he never put down a book; by day he studied military strategy, by night he pondered Confucian classics. Whenever ascending high places, he composed poetry, and when creating new verses, they were set to music and became musical compositions. His talent and strength surpassed ordinary men; he could shoot flying birds with his own hands, personally hunt fierce beasts, and once shot sixty-three pheasants in a single day at Nanpi. When constructing palaces or repairing military equipment, he established rules and standards for everything, fully expressing his intent in each endeavor. He naturally possessed a frugal character and disliked extravagance. The palace ladies did not wear brocade or embroidery; the attendants' shoes were of simple design, with no elaborate patterns. When curtains, screens, or bed hangings became damaged, they were mended rather than replaced. Mattresses and bedding were chosen for warmth alone, without any ornamental embellishments. When capturing cities and seizing beautiful or luxurious items, he would bestow them all upon those who had rendered meritorious service. If someone's contributions deserved reward, he was not stingy with thousands of gold pieces; if there was no merit, even the smallest favor was withheld. All tributes from across the realm were shared with his subordinates. He often considered that the elaborate funeral rites and excessive mourning customs were complicated yet of no real benefit, with common practices even exceeding them. Therefore, he prepared in advance for his own death, making only four simple caskets of clothing.

125
Jump to dictionary
武帝紀:
傅子曰：太祖愍嫁娶之奢僭，公女適人，皆以皁帳，從婢不過十人。
Fu Zi said: The Taizu was concerned about the excessive extravagance in marriage customs. When his daughters married, they were given only plain black curtains and no more than ten female attendants to accompany them.

126
Jump to dictionary
武帝紀:
張華博物志曰：漢世，安平崔瑗、瑗子寔、弘農張芝、芝弟昶並善草書，而太祖亞之。桓譚、蔡邕善音樂，馮翊山子道、王九真、郭凱等善圍棊，太祖皆與埒能。又好養性法，亦解方藥，招引方術之士，廬江左慈、譙郡華他、甘陵甘始、陽城郄儉無不畢至，又習啖野葛至一尺，亦得少多飲鴆酒。
The Zhan Hua Bo Wu Zhi states: In the Han dynasty, Cui Yuan of Anping and his son Shi, Zhang Zhi of Hongnong and his younger brother Chang were all skilled in cursive script, but the Taizu was second to none among them. Huan Tan and Cai Yong were skilled in music, while Shan Zidao of Fengyi, Wang Jiuzhen, Guo Kai, and others excelled at Go. The Taizu was on equal footing with all of them in these abilities. He also had a strong interest in health-preserving techniques and was knowledgeable about herbal medicine. He invited scholars of alchemy and longevity, including Zuo Ci from Lujiang, Hua Ta from Qiao Jun, Gan Shi from Ganling, and Xie Jian from Yangcheng—none failed to arrive. He practiced eating wild yew roots up to a foot long and could also consume small or moderate amounts of arsenic-laced wine.

127
Jump to dictionary
武帝紀:
傅子曰：漢末王公，多委王服，以幅巾為雅，是以袁紹、崔豹之徒，雖為將帥，皆著縑巾。魏太祖以天下凶荒，資財乏匱，擬古皮弁，裁縑帛以為帢，合于簡易隨時之義，以色別其貴賤，于今施行，可謂軍容，非國容也。
Fu Zi said: At the end of the Han dynasty, many aristocrats abandoned official robes and considered plain headscarves to be elegant. Thus, figures like Yuan Shao and Cui Bao, though generals, all wore simple silk head coverings. The Taizu of Wei, seeing the widespread famine and scarcity of resources across the land, imitated the ancient pi bian (a type of ceremonial cap) by cutting silk fabric to make head coverings. This practice aligned with the principle of simplicity and adaptability in accordance with circumstances. He used colors to distinguish rank and status; this system was implemented from that time onward. It could be considered suitable for military dress, but not for formal state ceremonies.

128
Jump to dictionary
武帝紀:
曹瞞傳曰：太祖為人佻易無威重，好音樂，倡優在側，常以日達夕。被服輕綃，身自佩小鞶囊，以盛手巾細物，時或冠帢帽以見賔客。每與人談論，戲弄言誦，盡無所隱，及歡恱大笑，至以頭沒柸案中，肴膳皆沾洿巾幘，其輕易如此。然持法峻刻，諸將有計畫勝出己者，隨以法誅之，及故人舊怨，亦皆無餘。其所刑殺，輒對之垂涕嗟痛之，終無所活。初，袁忠為沛相，甞欲以法治太祖，沛國桓邵亦輕之，及在兖州，陳留邊讓言議頗侵太祖，太祖殺讓，族其家。忠、邵俱避難交州，太祖遣使就太守止燮盡族之。桓邵得出首，拜謝於庭中，太祖謂曰：「跪可解死邪！」遂殺之。甞出軍，行經麥中，令「士卒無敗麥，犯者死」。騎士皆下馬，付麥以相付，於是太祖馬騰入麥中，勑主簿議罪；主簿對以春秋之義，罰不加於尊。太祖曰：「制法而自犯之，何以帥下？然孤為軍帥，不可自殺，請自刑。」因援劔割髮以置地。又有幸姬常從晝寢，枕之卧，告之曰：「須臾覺我。」姬見太祖卧安，未即寤，及自覺，棒殺之。常討賊，廩穀不足，私謂主者曰：「如何？」主者曰：「可以小斛以足之。」太祖曰：「善。」後軍中言太祖欺衆，太祖謂主者曰：「特當借君死以猒衆，不然事不解。」乃斬之，取首題徇曰：「行小斛，盜官穀，斬之軍門。」其酷虐變詐，皆此之類也。
The Biography of Cao Man said: The Taizu was a man who appeared light-hearted and lacking in solemnity, fond of music. Entertainers were always by his side, and he often enjoyed performances from day until night. He wore light, plain garments and personally carried a small sash pouch to hold handkerchiefs and other small items. At times, he would wear a simple head covering when receiving guests. Whenever he conversed with others, he would jest and recite casually, revealing nothing in reserve. When laughing heartily, he sometimes buried his head into the table, spilling food onto his cap and head covering; such was his casual manner. Yet he enforced the law with severity; if any general devised a strategy superior to his own, they were executed according to legal provisions. Old grudges and personal vendettas against friends or acquaintances also left no survivors. Whenever he carried out punishments or executions, he would weep before the condemned and express sorrowful lamentations, yet in the end no one was spared. Initially, Yuan Zhong served as the magistrate of Pei and once intended to punish the Taizu according to law. Huan Shao of Pei Guo also looked down on him. When the Taizu later governed Yan Zhou, Bian Rang of Chenliu made remarks that greatly offended him; in response, the Taizu killed Bian Rang and exterminated his entire family. Yuan Zhong and Huan Shao both fled to Jiaozhou for refuge, but the Taizu sent envoys to the governor Zhi Xie, ordering them to exterminate their entire families. Huan Shao managed to surrender first and kowtowed in the courtyard, offering thanks. The Taizu said to him, "Will kneeling save your life?" He was then executed. Once, when leading an army campaign, he passed through a field of wheat and issued the order: "Soldiers must not trample the wheat; those who do shall be put to death." The cavalrymen all dismounted and carefully carried the wheat aside. However, the Taizu's horse suddenly bolted into the field of wheat. He then ordered his chief clerk to deliberate on the punishment. The chief clerk cited the principles from the Spring and Autumn Annals, stating that penalties should not be imposed upon those of high rank. The Taizu said: "If I myself break the law I have established, how can I lead my subordinates?" "However, as a military commander, I cannot kill myself. Please allow me to punish myself." He then drew his sword and cut off his hair, placing it on the ground. There was also a favored concubine who often accompanied him during daytime naps; she would place her head on his pillow and sleep, telling him: "Wake me in a moment." When the concubine saw that the Taizu was sleeping soundly, she did not wake him immediately. When he finally awoke on his own, he beat her to death with a stick. During frequent campaigns against bandits, the stored grain was insufficient. He privately asked the official in charge: "What should we do?" The official replied: "We can use smaller measures to make up for it." The Taizu said, "Good." Later, when the army accused the Taizu of deceiving them, he told the official: "You must die to satisfy their anger; otherwise, this matter cannot be resolved." He then executed the official and displayed his head with a notice reading: "For using smaller measures and stealing government grain, he was beheaded at the military gate." His cruelty, harshness, and deceitful behavior were all of this nature.

武帝紀:
評曰：漢末，天下大亂，雄豪並起，而袁紹虎眎四州，彊盛莫敵。太祖運籌演謀，鞭撻宇內，擥申、商之法術，該韓、白之奇策，官方授材，各因其器，矯情任筭，不念舊惡，終能總御皇機，克成洪業者，惟其明略最優也。抑可謂非常之人，超世之傑矣。
Commentary: At the end of the Han, when chaos engulfed the land and powerful men arose together, Yuan Shao regarded four states as his domain, his strength unmatched. The Taizu planned and strategized with skill, wielding his influence across the realm. He employed the legalist methods of Shen Buhai and Shang Yang, as well as the ingenious strategies of Han Fei and Bai Qi. He appointed officials according to their abilities, adapting to each person's strengths; he controlled emotions for practical purposes and did not dwell on past grievances. In the end, he was able to grasp imperial authority and accomplish great achievements—this was due solely to his exceptional wisdom and strategic insight. Indeed, he may be called an extraordinary man, a peerless hero beyond the ordinary world.
